**TASK DESCRIPTION:**
Vector Outer Product

Calculate the outer product of two vectors. Given two one-dimensional numerical vectors, the outer product results in a two-dimensional matrix where each element in the i-th row and j-th column is the product of the i-th element of the first vector and the j-th element of the second vector.

Input:
A tuple containing two arrays, vec1 and vec2, representing the two input vectors. These vectors will have the same length n.

Example input:
([1, 2, 3], [4, 5, 6])

Output:
A NumPy array representing the outer product of the two input vectors. The output will be an n x n matrix.

Example output:
[[ 4., 5., 6.],
[ 8., 10., 12.],
[12., 15., 18.]]

Category: matrix_operations

Below is the reference implementation. Your function should run much quicker.

import numpy as np

| 1: def solve(self, problem: tuple[np.ndarray, np.ndarray]) -> np.ndarray:
| 2:     vec1, vec2 = problem
| 3:     outer_product = np.outer(vec1, vec2)
| 4:     return outer_product
| 5: 

This function will be used to check if your solution is valid for a given problem. If it returns False, it means the solution is invalid:

import numpy as np

| 01: def is_solution( problem: tuple[np.ndarray, np.ndarray], solution: np.ndarray) -> bool:
| 02:     vec1, vec2 = problem
| 03:     reference = np.outer(vec1, vec2)
| 04:     tol = 1e-6
| 05:     error = (
| 06:         2
| 07:         * np.linalg.norm(solution - reference)
| 08:         / (np.linalg.norm(reference + solution) + 1e-12)
| 09:     )
| 10:     if error > tol:
| 11:         logging.error(f"Vector outer product error {error} exceeds tolerance {tol}.")
| 12:         return False
| 13:     return True

Vector Outer Product

Calculate the outer product of two vectors. Given two one-dimensional numerical vectors, the outer product results in a two-dimensional matrix where each element in the i-th row and j-th column is the product of the i-th element of the first vector and the j-th element of the second vector.

Input:
A tuple containing two arrays, vec1 and vec2, representing the two input vectors. These vectors will have the same length n.

Example input:
([1, 2, 3], [4, 5, 6])

Output:
A NumPy array representing the outer product of the two input vectors. The output will be an n x n matrix.

Example output:
[[ 4., 5., 6.],
[ 8., 10., 12.],
[12., 15., 18.]]

Category: matrix_operations
Reference Implementation 
import numpy as np

 def solve(self, problem: tuple[np.ndarray, np.ndarray]) -> np.ndarray:
     vec1, vec2 = problem
     outer_product = np.outer(vec1, vec2)
     return outer_product

import numpy as np

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        return vec1.reshape(-1, 1) * vec2

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
We'll use Numba to parallelize the outer product computation. Since the problem involves numerical vectors and the operation is embarrassingly parallel, we can achieve significant speedups by:
1. Pre-compiling a Numba function with parallel=True
2. Making arrays contiguous for efficient memory access
3. Using a fallback to NumPy's outer for unsupported types

The implementation will:
- Precompile for common dtypes in __init__
- Use parallel loops for computation
- Handle non-contiguous arrays by making copies
- Fall back to NumPy for unsupported cases

Let's create the solver.py file with this optimized implementation.<｜tool▁calls▁begin｜><｜tool▁call▁begin｜>function<｜tool▁sep｜>edit
edit
file: solver.py
lines: 0-0
---
import numpy as np
from numba import njit, prange
import logging

class Solver:
    def __init__(self):
        self.supported_dtypes = [np.float64, np.int64, np.float32, np.int32]
        # Precompile for supported dtypes
        for dtype in self.supported_dtypes:
            arr1 = np.array([1, 2], dtype=dtype)
            arr2 = np.array([3, 4], dtype=dtype)
            try:
                _ = self._outer(arr1, arr2)
            except Exception as e:
                logging.warning(f"Precompilation for dtype {dtype} failed: {e}")

    @staticmethod
    @njit(parallel=True, cache=True)
    def _outer(vec1, vec2):
        n = vec1.size
        m = vec2.size
        out = np.empty((n, m))
        for i in prange(n):
            for j in range(m):
                out[i, j] = vec1[i] * vec2[j]
        return out

    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        # Handle same dtype in supported list
        if vec1.dtype == vec2.dtype and vec1.dtype in self.supported_dtypes:
            # Ensure contiguous memory layout
            if not vec1.flags.contiguous:
                vec1 = np.ascontiguousarray(vec1)
            if not vec2.flags.contiguous:
                vec2 = np.ascontiguousarray(vec2)
            try:
                return self._outer(vec1, vec2)
            except Exception as e:
                logging.warning(f"Numba outer failed: {e}, falling back to numpy")
        # Fallback to numpy for unsupported cases
        return np.outer(vec1, vec2)

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The current implementation using vectorized broadcasting is already 2.61x faster than the reference solution. However, we can further optimize by:

1. Ensuring arrays are contiguous in memory
2. Using explicit output pre-allocation to avoid temporary arrays
3. Using the `np.multiply` ufunc with broadcasting for maximum efficiency

Let's implement these optimizations:<｜tool▁calls▁begin｜><｜tool▁call▁begin｜>function<｜tool▁sep｜>edit
edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-7
---
import numpy as np

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        # Ensure contiguous arrays for better memory access
        vec1 = np.ascontiguousarray(vec1)
        vec2 = np.ascontiguousarray(vec2)
        # Pre-allocate output array and use explicit multiplication
        result = np.empty((len(vec1), len(vec2)), dtype=vec1.dtype)
        np.multiply(vec1[:, np.newaxis], vec2, out=result)
        return result

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The current implementation achieves a 3.09x speedup. To further optimize:

1. Use BLAS through SciPy for float32/float64 operations (highly optimized)
2. Maintain the efficient broadcasting method for other data types
3. Pre-import SciPy modules in __init__ to avoid runtime overhead

Here's the optimized implementation:<｜tool▁calls▁begin｜><｜tool▁call▁begin｜>function<｜tool▁sep｜>edit

import numpy as np
from scipy.linalg.blas import sger, dger

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        n, m = len(vec1), len(vec2)
        
        # Use optimized BLAS for float32/float64
        if vec1.dtype == np.float32 and vec2.dtype == np.float32:
            return sger(1.0, vec1, vec2, a=np.zeros((n, m), dtype=np.float32, order='F'))
        elif vec1.dtype == np.float64 and vec2.dtype == np.float64:
            return dger(1.0, vec1, vec2, a=np.zeros((n, m), dtype=np.float64, order='F'))
        
        # Use efficient broadcasting for other types
        result = np.empty((n, m), dtype=vec1.dtype)
        np.multiply(vec1[:, np.newaxis], vec2, out=result)
        return result

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The previous BLAS approach had import issues. Let's try using SciPy's `get_blas_funcs` to dynamically load the optimized GER functions (rank-1 updates) for float32/float64. This avoids direct import problems while leveraging BLAS optimizations.

Here's the revised implementation:<｜tool▁calls▁begin｜><｜tool▁call▁begin｜>function<｜tool▁sep｜>edit
edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-12
---
import numpy as np
from scipy.linalg import get_blas_funcs

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        n, m = len(vec1), len(vec2)
        
        # Use optimized BLAS for float32/float64
        if vec1.dtype in (np.float32, np.float64) and vec2.dtype == vec1.dtype:
            ger = get_blas_funcs('ger', (vec1, vec2))
            # Create Fortran-order matrix for efficient BLAS operation
            result = np.zeros((n, m), dtype=vec1.dtype, order='F')
            ger(1.0, vec1, vec2, a=result, overwrite_a=1)
            return result
        
        # Use efficient broadcasting for other types
        result = np.empty((n, m), dtype=vec1.dtype)
        np.multiply(vec1[:, None], vec2, out=result)
        return result
---

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The BLAS approach didn't improve performance. Let's revert to the efficient broadcasting method and further optimize it by:

1. Avoiding unnecessary array copies by only making arrays contiguous when needed
2. Using direct multiplication instead of `np.multiply`
3. Pre-calculating array lengths to avoid repeated function calls

Here's the optimized version:<｜tool▁calls▁begin｜><｜tool▁call▁begin｜>function<｜tool▁sep｜>edit
edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-20
---
import numpy as np

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        n = len(vec1)
        m = len(vec2)
        
        # Only make copies if arrays aren't contiguous
        if not vec1.flags.contiguous:
            vec1 = np.ascontiguousarray(vec1)
        if not vec2.flags.contiguous:
            vec2 = np.ascontiguousarray(vec2)
            
        # Use efficient broadcasting with direct multiplication
        return vec1.reshape((n, 1)) * vec2

import numpy as np
cimport numpy as np
cimport cython

@cython.boundscheck(False)
@cython.wraparound(False)
cpdef np.ndarray[np.double_t, ndim=2] cython_outer(double[::1] vec1, double[::1] vec2):
    cdef int n = vec1.shape[0]
    cdef int m = vec2.shape[0]
    cdef np.ndarray[np.double_t, ndim=2] result = np.empty((n, m), dtype=np.double)
    cdef int i, j
    
    for i in range(n):
        for j in range(m):
            result[i, j] = vec1[i] * vec2[j]
    return result

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        # Convert to double for maximum performance
        vec1 = np.asarray(vec1, dtype=np.double, order='C')
        vec2 = np.asarray(vec2, dtype=np.double, order='C')
        return cython_outer(vec1, vec2)

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The Cython approach didn't provide significant improvement. Let's try Numba with parallelization for better performance:<｜tool▁calls▁begin｜><｜tool▁call▁begin｜>function<｜tool▁sep｜>edit

file: solver.py
lines: 1-16
---
import numpy as np
import numba

@numba.njit(parallel=True)
def numba_outer(vec1, vec2):
    n = vec1.shape[0]
    m = vec2.shape[0]
    result = np.empty((n, m), dtype=vec1.dtype)
    for i in numba.prange(n):
        for j in range(m):
            result[i, j] = vec1[i] * vec2[j]
    return result

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        # Ensure contiguous arrays for optimal Numba performance
        vec1 = np.ascontiguousarray(vec1)
        vec2 = np.ascontiguousarray(vec2)
        return numba_outer(vec1, vec2)
---

import numpy as np
from numba import njit, prange

@njit(parallel=True)
def numba_outer(vec1, vec2):
    n = vec1.shape[0]
    m = vec2.shape[0]
    result = np.empty((n, m), dtype=vec1.dtype)
    for i in prange(n):
        for j in range(m):
            result[i, j] = vec1[i] * vec2[j]
    return result

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        # Ensure contiguous arrays for optimal Numba performance
        vec1 = np.ascontiguousarray(vec1)
        vec2 = np.ascontiguousarray(vec2)
        return numba_outer(vec1, vec2)
---

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The error persists because the static linter doesn't recognize Numba's `prange`. Let's switch to a vectorized approach using PyTorch for GPU acceleration which can provide significant speedups:<｜tool▁calls▁begin｜><｜tool▁call▁begin｜>function<｜tool▁sep｜>edit
edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-16
---
import numpy as np
import torch

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        
        # Convert to PyTorch tensors and move to GPU if available
        device = torch.device("cuda" if torch.cuda.is_available() else "cpu")
        t1 = torch.tensor(vec1, device=device)
        t2 = torch.tensor(vec2, device=device)
        
        # Compute outer product on GPU
        result = t1.unsqueeze(1) * t2
        
        # Convert back to numpy array on CPU
        return result.cpu().numpy()
---

The PyTorch approach didn't improve performance due to data transfer overhead. Let's try using Numba again but with a simpler implementation and proper imports:<｜tool▁calls▁begin｜><｜tool▁call▁begin｜>function<｜tool▁sep｜>edit

file: solver.py
lines: 1-17
---
import numpy as np
from numba import jit

@jit(nopython=True, parallel=True)
def outer_product(vec1, vec2):
    n = vec1.size
    m = vec2.size
    result = np.empty((n, m), dtype=vec1.dtype)
    for i in range(n):
        for j in range(m):
            result[i, j] = vec1[i] * vec2[j]
    return result

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        return outer_product(vec1, vec2)
---

- `reference <string>`: Query the reference solver with a problem and receive its solution. If the problem's input is a list, this command would look like:
```
reference [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval_input <string>`: Run your current solver implementation on the given input. This is the only command that shows stdout from your solver along with both solutions. Example:
```
eval_input [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval`: Run evaluation on the current solution and report the results.
- `delete`: Delete a range of lines from a file using the format:
```
delete
file: <file_name>
lines: <start_line>-<end_line>

The command will delete the lines from <start_line> to <end_line> (inclusive)

Example:
```
delete
file: solver.py
lines: 5-10
```
- `profile <filename.py> <input>`: Profile your currently loaded solve method's performance on a given input. Shows the 25 most time-consuming lines. Requires specifying a python file (e.g., `solver.py`) for validation, though profiling runs on the current in-memory code.
Example:
```
profile solver.py [1, 2, 3]
```
- `profile_lines <filename.py> <line_number1, line_number2, ...> <input>`: Profiles the chosen lines of the currently loaded code on the given input. Requires specifying a python file for validation.
Example:
```
profile_lines solver.py 1,2,3 [1, 2, 3]
```

- `edit`: Replace a range of lines with new content in a file. This is how you can create files: if the file does not exist, it will be created. Here is an example:
```
edit
file: <file_name>
lines: <start_line>-<end_line>
---
<new_content>
---
```

The command will:
1. Delete the lines from <start_line> to <end_line> (inclusive)
2. Insert <new_content> starting at <start_line>
3. If both <start_line> and <end_line> are 0, <new_content> will be prepended to the file

Example:
edit
file: solver.py
lines: 5-7
---
def improved_function():
print("Optimized solution")
---
- `ls`: List all files in the current working directory.
- `view_file <file_name> [start_line]`: Display 100 lines of `<file_name>` starting from `start_line` (defaults to line 1).
- `revert`: Revert the code to the best-performing version thus far.
- `reference <string>`: Query the reference solver with a problem and receive its solution. If the problem's input is a list, this command would look like:
```
reference [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval_input <string>`: Run your current solver implementation on the given input. This is the only command that shows stdout from your solver along with both solutions. Example:
```
eval_input [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval`: Run evaluation on the current solution and report the results.
- `delete`: Delete a range of lines from a file using the format:
```
delete
file: <file_name>
lines: <start_line>-<end_line>

The command will delete the lines from <start_line> to <end_line> (inclusive)

Example:
```
delete
file: solver.py
lines: 5-10
```
- `profile <filename.py> <input>`: Profile your currently loaded solve method's performance on a given input. Shows the 25 most time-consuming lines. Requires specifying a python file (e.g., `solver.py`) for validation, though profiling runs on the current in-memory code.
Example:
```
profile solver.py [1, 2, 3]
```
- `profile_lines <filename.py> <line_number1, line_number2, ...> <input>`: Profiles the chosen lines of the currently loaded code on the given input. Requires specifying a python file for validation.
Example:
```
profile_lines solver.py 1,2,3 [1, 2, 3]
```

Here are the commands available to you. Ensure you include one and only one of the following commands in each of your responses:
- `edit`: Replace a range of lines with new content in a file. This is how you can create files: if the file does not exist, it will be created. Here is an example:
```
edit
file: <file_name>
lines: <start_line>-<end_line>
---
<new_content>
---
```

The command will:
1. Delete the lines from <start_line> to <end_line> (inclusive)
2. Insert <new_content> starting at <start_line>
3. If both <start_line> and <end_line> are 0, <new_content> will be prepended to the file

Example:
edit
file: solver.py
lines: 5-7
---
def improved_function():
print("Optimized solution")
---
- `ls`: List all files in the current working directory.
- `view_file <file_name> [start_line]`: Display 100 lines of `<file_name>` starting from `start_line` (defaults to line 1).
- `revert`: Revert the code to the best-performing version thus far.
- `reference <string>`: Query the reference solver with a problem and receive its solution. If the problem's input is a list, this command would look like:
```
reference [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval_input <string>`: Run your current solver implementation on the given input. This is the only command that shows stdout from your solver along with both solutions. Example:
```
eval_input [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval`: Run evaluation on the current solution and report the results.
- `delete`: Delete a range of lines from a file using the format:
```
delete
file: <file_name>
lines: <start_line>-<end_line>

The command will delete the lines from <start_line> to <end_line> (inclusive)

Example:
```
delete
file: solver.py
lines: 5-10
```
- `profile <filename.py> <input>`: Profile your currently loaded solve method's performance on a given input. Shows the 25 most time-consuming lines. Requires specifying a python file (e.g., `solver.py`) for validation, though profiling runs on the current in-memory code.
Example:
```
profile solver.py [1, 2, 3]
```
- `profile_lines <filename.py> <line_number1, line_number2, ...> <input>`: Profiles the chosen lines of the currently loaded code on the given input. Requires specifying a python file for validation.
Example:
```
profile_lines solver.py 1,2,3 [1, 2, 3]
```

eval_input [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval`: Run evaluation on the current solution and report the results.
- `delete`: Delete a range of lines from a file using the format:
```
delete
file: <file_name>
lines: <start_line>-<end_line>

The command will delete the lines from <start_line> to <end_line> (inclusive)

Example:
```
delete
file: solver.py
lines: 5-10
```
- `profile <filename.py> <input>`: Profile your currently loaded solve method's performance on a given input. Shows the 25 most time-consuming lines. Requires specifying a python file (e.g., `solver.py`) for validation, though profiling runs on the current in-memory code.
Example:
```
profile solver.py [1, 2, 3]
```
- `profile_lines <filename.py> <line_number1, line_number2, ...> <input>`: Profiles the chosen lines of the currently loaded code on the given input. Requires specifying a python file for validation.
Example:
```
profile_lines solver.py 1,2,3 [1, 2, 3]
```

- `ls`: List all files in the current working directory.
- `view_file <file_name> [start_line]`: Display 100 lines of `<file_name>` starting from `start_line` (defaults to line 1).
- `revert`: Revert the code to the best-performing version thus far.
- `reference <string>`: Query the reference solver with a problem and receive its solution. If the problem's input is a list, this command would look like:
```
reference [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval_input <string>`: Run your current solver implementation on the given input. This is the only command that shows stdout from your solver along with both solutions. Example:
```
eval_input [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval`: Run evaluation on the current solution and report the results.
- `delete`: Delete a range of lines from a file using the format:
```
delete
file: <file_name>
lines: <start_line>-<end_line>

The command will delete the lines from <start_line> to <end_line> (inclusive)

Example:
```
delete
file: solver.py
lines: 5-10
```
- `profile <filename.py> <input>`: Profile your currently loaded solve method's performance on a given input. Shows the 25 most time-consuming lines. Requires specifying a python file (e.g., `solver.py`) for validation, though profiling runs on the current in-memory code.
Example:
```
profile solver.py [1, 2, 3]
```
- `profile_lines <filename.py> <line_number1, line_number2, ...> <input>`: Profiles the chosen lines of the currently loaded code on the given input. Requires specifying a python file for validation.
Example:
```
profile_lines solver.py 1,2,3 [1, 2, 3]
```

Here are the commands available to you. Ensure you include one and only one of the following commands in each of your responses:
- `edit`: Replace a range of lines with new content in a file. This is how you can create files: if the file does not exist, it will be created. Here is an example:
```
edit
file: <file_name>
lines: <start_line>-<end_line>
---
<new_content>
---
```

The command will:
1. Delete the lines from <start_line> to <end_line> (inclusive)
2. Insert <new_content> starting at <start_line>
3. If both <start_line> and <end_line> are 0, <new_content> will be prepended to the file

- `ls`: List all files in the current working directory.
- `view_file <file_name> [start_line]`: Display 100 lines of `<file_name>` starting from `start_line` (defaults to line 1).
- `revert`: Revert the code to the best-performing version thus far.
- `reference <string>`: Query the reference solver with a problem and receive its solution. If the problem's input is a list, this command would look like:
```
reference [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval_input <string>`: Run your current solver implementation on the given input. This is the only command that shows stdout from your solver along with both solutions. Example:
```
eval_input [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval`: Run evaluation on the current solution and report the results.
- `delete`: Delete a range of lines from a file using the format:
```
delete
file: <file_name>
lines: <start_line>-<end_line>

The command will delete the lines from <start_line> to <end_line> (inclusive)

Example:
```
delete
file: solver.py
lines: 5-10
```
- `profile <filename.py> <input>`: Profile your currently loaded solve method's performance on a given input. Shows the 25 most time-consuming lines. Requires specifying a python file (e.g., `solver.py`) for validation, though profiling runs on the current in-memory code.
Example:
```
profile solver.py [1, 2, 3]
```
- `profile_lines <filename.py> <line_number1, line_number2, ...> <input>`: Profiles the chosen lines of the currently loaded code on the given input. Requires specifying a python file for validation.
Example:
```
profile_lines solver.py 1,2,3 [1, 2, 3]
```

- `ls`: List all files in the current working directory.
- `view_file <file_name> [start_line]`: Display 100 lines of `<file_name>` starting from `start_line` (defaults to line 1).
- `revert`: Revert the code to the best-performing version thus far.
- `reference <string>`: Query the reference solver with a problem and receive its solution. If the problem's input is a list, this command would look like:
```
reference [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval_input <string>`: Run your current solver implementation on the given input. This is the only command that shows stdout from your solver along with both solutions. Example:
```
eval_input [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval`: Run evaluation on the current solution and report the results.

delete
file: <file_name>

- `profile <filename.py> <input>`: Profile your currently loaded solve method's performance on a given input. Shows the 25 most time-consuming lines. Requires specifying a python file (e.g., `solver.py`) for validation, though profiling runs on the current in-memory code.
Example:
```
profile solver.py [1, 2, 3]
```
- `profile_lines <filename.py> <line_number1, line_number2, ...> <input>`: Profiles the chosen lines of the currently loaded code on the given input. Requires specifying a python file for validation.

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The current implementation using broadcasting is already 1.05x faster than the reference solution. To further optimize, let's try using Numba with a simple loop implementation without parallelization to avoid linter issues:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-6
---
import numpy as np
from numba import jit

@jit(nopython=True)
def outer_product(vec1, vec2):
    n = vec1.size
    m = vec2.size
    result = np.empty((n, m), dtype=vec1.dtype)
    for i in range(n):
        for j in range(m):
            result[i, j] = vec1[i] * vec2[j]
    return result

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        return outer_product(vec1, vec2)
---

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The Numba implementation achieved a 1.16x speedup. To further optimize, let's enable parallelization and use `prange` for the outer loop:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 4-12

@jit(nopython=True, parallel=True)
def outer_product(vec1, vec2):
    n = vec1.size
    m = vec2.size
    result = np.empty((n, m), dtype=vec1.dtype)
    for i in numba.prange(n):
        for j in range(m):
            result[i, j] = vec1[i] * vec2[j]
    return result

        vec1, vec2 = problem
        # Ensure contiguous arrays for better memory access

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The broadcasting method is now performing at the same level as the reference solution. Let's try a different optimization approach using Cython. We'll create a Cython module for the outer product calculation:

First, create the Cython file:

edit
file: outer_product.pyx
lines: 0-0
---
# distutils: language=c++
# distutils: extra_compile_args = -O3 -march=native -ffast-math
# distutils: extra_link_args = -O3 -march=native -ffast-math

import numpy as np
cimport numpy as np
cimport cython
from cython.parallel import prange

@cython.boundscheck(False)
@cython.wraparound(False)
cpdef np.ndarray[np.double_t, ndim=2] cython_outer(double[:] vec1, double[:] vec2):
    cdef int i, j
    cdef int n = vec1.shape[0]
    cdef int m = vec2.shape[0]
    cdef np.ndarray[np.double_t, ndim=2] result = np.empty((n, m), dtype=np.float64)
    
    for i in prange(n, nogil=True):
        for j in range(m):
            result[i, j] = vec1[i] * vec2[j]
    return result
---


Now create a setup.py to compile the Cython module:

edit
file: setup.py
lines: 0-0
---
from distutils.core import setup
from Cython.Build import cythonize
import numpy

setup(
    ext_modules=cythonize("outer_product.pyx"),
    include_dirs=[numpy.get_include()]
)
---

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The Cython module import issue persists. Let me try a different approach by embedding the Cython code directly in solver.py using pyximport:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-6
---
import numpy as np
import pyximport
pyximport.install(setup_args={"include_dirs": np.get_include()})

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        # Cython implementation embedded in docstring
        code = """
        # distutils: language=c++
        # distutils: extra_compile_args = -O3 -march=native -ffast-math
        # distutils: extra_link_args = -O3 -march=native -ffast-math
        
        import numpy as np
        cimport numpy as np
        cimport cython
        from cython.parallel import prange
        
        @cython.boundscheck(False)
        @cython.wraparound(False)
        def cython_outer(double[:] vec1, double[:] vec2):
            cdef int i, j, n = vec1.shape[0], m = vec2.shape[0]
            cdef np.ndarray[np.double_t, ndim=2] result = np.empty((n, m), dtype=np.float64)
            
            for i in prange(n, nogil=True):
                for j in range(m):
                    result[i, j] = vec1[i] * vec2[j]
            return result
        """
        from Cython import Tempita
        outer_module = Tempita.sub(code)
        from imp import new_module
        m = new_module("outer")
        exec(outer_module, m.__dict__)
        return m.cython_outer(vec1, vec2)
---

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The `np.multiply.outer` approach didn't provide significant improvement. Let's try using Numba JIT compilation to optimize the outer product calculation. Numba can compile Python code to machine code and often provides significant speedups for numerical operations:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-7
---
import numpy as np
from numba import njit

@njit(fastmath=True)
def outer_product(vec1, vec2):
    n = len(vec1)
    m = len(vec2)
    result = np.empty((n, m), dtype=np.float64)
    for i in range(n):
        for j in range(m):
            result[i, j] = vec1[i] * vec2[j]
    return result

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        return outer_product(vec1, vec2)
---

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The Numba JIT implementation achieved a 1.28x speedup. To further optimize, let's enable parallel execution and use `prange` for the outer loop:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-17
---
import numpy as np
from numba import njit, prange

@njit(fastmath=True, parallel=True)
def outer_product(vec1, vec2):
    n = len(vec1)
    m = len(vec2)
    result = np.empty((n, m), dtype=np.float64)
    for i in prange(n):
        for j in range(m):
            result[i, j] = vec1[i] * vec2[j]
    return result

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        return outer_product(vec1, vec2)
---

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The error occurs because the static linter doesn't recognize Numba's `prange` as an iterable. Let me fix this by using a different approach that will satisfy the linter while maintaining parallel execution:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-17
---
import numpy as np
from numba import njit, prange

@njit(fastmath=True, parallel=True)
def outer_product(vec1, vec2):
    n = len(vec1)
    m = len(vec2)
    result = np.empty((n, m), dtype=np.float64)
    # Using range for linting compatibility
    for i in prange(n):  # prange will work at runtime
        for j in range(m):
            result[i, j] = vec1[i] * vec2[j]
    return result

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        return outer_product(vec1, vec2)

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The issue is that the static linter doesn't recognize Numba's `prange` as an iterable. Let me modify the approach to use a regular range in a way that satisfies the linter, while still maintaining parallel execution:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-17
---
import numpy as np
from numba import njit, prange

@njit(fastmath=True, parallel=True)
def outer_product(vec1, vec2):
    n = len(vec1)
    m = len(vec2)
    result = np.empty((n, m), dtype=np.float64)
    # Use range for linting compatibility
    for i in range(n):
        # Inner loop remains the same
        for j in range(m):
            result[i, j] = vec1[i] * vec2[j]
    return result

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        return outer_product(vec1, vec2)

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The parallel implementation using `prange` is actually faster, but the linter is preventing us from using it. However, we achieved a 3.05x speedup with the regular range implementation. To further optimize, let's try using Numba's `@guvectorize` which is designed for generalized universal functions and can provide better performance for array operations:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-19
---
import numpy as np
from numba import guvectorize, float64

@guvectorize([(float64[:], float64[:], float64[:,:])], '(n),(m)->(n,m)', nopython=True, fastmath=True)
def outer_product(vec1, vec2, result):
    n = vec1.shape[0]
    m = vec2.shape[0]
    for i in range(n):
        for j in range(m):
            result[i, j] = vec1[i] * vec2[j]

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        # Initialize the result array
        result = np.empty((len(vec1), len(vec2)), dtype=np.float64)
        outer_product(vec1, vec2, result)
        return result
---

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The current broadcasting implementation is efficient, but we can try to further optimize by ensuring contiguous arrays and using a more direct multiplication approach. Let's modify the implementation:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-7
---
import numpy as np

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        # Ensure contiguous arrays for better cache performance
        vec1 = np.ascontiguousarray(vec1)
        vec2 = np.ascontiguousarray(vec2)
        # Efficient broadcasting with precomputed dimensions
        return vec1.reshape(-1, 1) * vec2
---
This version ensures memory-contiguous arrays which can improve cache performance, and uses reshape instead of None for slightly faster dimension manipulation.

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The `np.multiply.outer` approach didn't improve performance. Let's try a different approach using Numba JIT compilation with parallel execution:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-7
---
import numpy as np
import numba

@numba.njit(parallel=True)
def outer_product(vec1, vec2):
    n = vec1.size
    m = vec2.size
    result = np.empty((n, m))
    for i in numba.prange(n):
        for j in numba.prange(m):
            result[i, j] = vec1[i] * vec2[j]
    return result

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        return outer_product(vec1, vec2)
---
This implementation uses Numba's parallel JIT compilation to optimize the double loop for outer product calculation.

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The linter is still having issues with `numba.prange`. Let me modify the implementation to use parallel execution only for the outer loop, which should be sufficient for parallelization:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-7
---
import numpy as np
import numba

@numba.njit(parallel=True)
def outer_product(vec1, vec2):
    n = vec1.size
    m = vec2.size
    result = np.empty((n, m))
    for i in numba.prange(n):
        for j in range(m):
            result[i, j] = vec1[i] * vec2[j]
    return result

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        return outer_product(vec1, vec2)
---
This implementation uses parallel execution only for the outer loop, which should avoid the linter errors while still providing parallelization benefits.

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-7
---
import numpy as np
import numba

@numba.vectorize(['float64(float64, float64)'], nopython=True)
def multiply(a, b):
    return a * b

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        return multiply(vec1[:, np.newaxis], vec2)
---

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The vectorize approach achieved a 2.74x speedup. To further optimize, let's try using Numba's `guvectorize` decorator which allows us to specify the output dimensions explicitly:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-11
---
import numpy as np
import numba

@numba.guvectorize(['(float64[:], float64[:], float64[:,:])'], '(n),(m)->(n,m)', nopython=True)
def outer_product(a, b, out):
    for i in range(a.shape[0]):
        for j in range(b.shape[0]):
            out[i, j] = a[i] * b[j]

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        result = np.empty((vec1.size, vec2.size))
        outer_product(vec1, vec2, result)
        return result
---
This implementation uses Numba's generalized universal functions which can be more efficient for matrix operations.

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The `guvectorize` approach achieved a 2.84x speedup. To further optimize, let's try using Numba's `stencil` decorator which is designed for grid-based operations and can provide efficient memory access patterns:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-15
---
import numpy as np
import numba

@numba.stencil
def outer_stencil(a, b):
    return a[0] * b[0]

@numba.njit(parallel=True)
def outer_product(vec1, vec2):
    n = vec1.size
    m = vec2.size
    result = np.empty((n, m))
    for i in numba.prange(n):
        result[i] = outer_stencil(vec1[i], vec2)
    return result

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        return outer_product(vec1, vec2)
---
This implementation uses Numba's stencil decorator to define the element-wise multiplication operation, then applies it efficiently across the vectors.

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The `guvectorize` approach achieved a 2.84x speedup. To further optimize, let's modify the implementation to use Numba's `njit` with parallel execution and vectorized row operations, which should reduce overhead:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-15
---
import numpy as np
import numba

@numba.njit(parallel=True)
def outer_product(vec1, vec2):
    n = vec1.size
    m = vec2.size
    result = np.empty((n, m))
    for i in numba.prange(n):
        result[i] = vec1[i] * vec2
    return result

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        return outer_product(vec1, vec2)
---
This implementation uses a single parallel loop over the rows, with each row being computed as a vectorized operation (scalar-vector multiplication), which should be more efficient than the double-nested loop.

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The issue is that the static linter doesn't recognize Numba's `prange` as an iterable. Let's try a different approach using Numba's `vectorize` decorator with explicit parallelization:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-15
---
import numpy as np
import numba

@numba.vectorize(['float64(float64, float64)'], nopython=True)
def multiply(a, b):
    return a * b

@numba.njit(parallel=True)
def outer_product(vec1, vec2):
    n = vec1.size
    m = vec2.size
    result = np.empty((n, m))
    for i in range(n):
        result[i] = multiply(vec1[i], vec2)
    return result

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        return outer_product(vec1, vec2)
---
This approach uses vectorization for the multiplication operation and parallel execution for the outer loop.

import numpy as np
import numba

@numba.vectorize(['float64(float64, float64)'], nopython=True)
def multiply(a, b):
    return a * b

@numba.njit(parallel=True)
def outer_product(vec1, vec2):
    n = vec1.size
    m = vec2.size
    result = np.empty((n, m))
    for i in range(n):
        result[i] = multiply(vec1[i], vec2)
    return result

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        return outer_product(vec1, vec2)

import numpy as np
import numba
from numba import prange

@numba.njit(parallel=True)
def outer_product(vec1, vec2):
    n = vec1.size
    m = vec2.size
    result = np.empty((n, m))
    for i in prange(n):
        for j in range(m):
            result[i, j] = vec1[i] * vec2[j]
    return result

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        return outer_product(vec1, vec2)
---

import numpy as np
import numba

@numba.njit(parallel=True)
def outer_product(vec1, vec2):
    n = vec1.size
    m = vec2.size
    result = np.empty((n, m))

        result[i] = vec1[i] * vec2
    return result

class Solver:

        vec1, vec2 = problem

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The issue with `prange` persists. Let's try a different approach using Numba's `vectorize` and `guvectorize` for parallel execution:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-20
---
import numpy as np
import numba

@numba.vectorize(['float64(float64, float64)'], nopython=True)
def multiply(a, b):
    return a * b

@numba.guvectorize(['(float64[:], float64[:], float64[:])'], '(n),(m)->(n,m)', nopython=True, parallel=True)
def outer_product_row(vec1, vec2, out):
    for j in range(vec2.size):
        out[j] = multiply(vec1[0], vec2[j])

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        n = vec1.size
        result = np.empty((n, vec2.size))
        for i in range(n):
            # Process each row in parallel
            outer_product_row(vec1[i:i+1], vec2, result[i])
        return result
---

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The error occurred because the `guvectorize` decorator doesn't support the `parallel` option. Let's fix this by using a different approach that combines Numba's `njit` with parallel execution for the row processing:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-21
---
import numpy as np
import numba

@numba.njit
def compute_row(i, vec1, vec2, result):
    for j in range(vec2.size):
        result[i, j] = vec1[i] * vec2[j]

@numba.njit(parallel=True)
def outer_product(vec1, vec2):
    n = vec1.size
    m = vec2.size
    result = np.empty((n, m))
    for i in numba.prange(n):
        compute_row(i, vec1, vec2, result)
    return result

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        return outer_product(vec1, vec2)
---
This approach uses a helper function for row computation and parallel execution of rows.

import numpy as np
import numba

@numba.njit
def compute_row(i, vec1, vec2, result):
    for j in range(vec2.size):
        result[i, j] = vec1[i] * vec2[j]

    return result

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The persistent issues with the linter and Numba's parallel features indicate we need a simpler approach. Let's revert to the efficient broadcasting method that previously showed good performance (3.31x speedup). This implementation is both concise and fast:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-21
---
import numpy as np

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        # Efficient broadcasting for outer product
        return vec1[:, None] * vec2
---

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The `np.multiply.outer` approach provides a slight improvement to 3.35x speedup. To further optimize, let's try using Numba's JIT compilation with parallel execution for the outer product calculation. We'll implement a nested loop with parallelization of the outer loop:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-7
---
import numpy as np
import numba

@numba.njit(parallel=True)
def outer_product(vec1, vec2):
    n = len(vec1)
    m = len(vec2)
    result = np.empty((n, m))
    for i in numba.prange(n):
        for j in range(m):
            result[i, j] = vec1[i] * vec2[j]
    return result

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        return outer_product(vec1, vec2)
---

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The contiguous arrays with `np.multiply.outer` achieved a 3.36x speedup. To further optimize, let's try using the `out` parameter to avoid creating a new array, and also use precomputation to avoid temporary arrays:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-10
---
import numpy as np

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        # Preallocate output array and compute in-place
        n = len(vec1)
        m = len(vec2)
        result = np.empty((n, m))
        # Use broadcasting with precomputation
        np.multiply(vec1[:, None], vec2, out=result)
        return result
---

import numpy as np
import torch

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        # Convert to PyTorch tensors and move to GPU

        t2 = torch.tensor(vec2, device='cuda')
        
        # Compute outer product on GPU
        result = t1.unsqueeze(1) * t2
        return result.cpu().numpy()

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The linter error is caused by the static analysis not recognizing `numba.prange` as an iterable. Let's modify the implementation to use a different parallelization approach that avoids `prange`. We'll use Numba's `vectorize` with parallel execution instead:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-13
---
import numpy as np
import numba

@numba.vectorize(['float64(float64, float64)'], nopython=True, target='parallel')
def multiply(a, b):
    return a * b

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        n = len(vec1)
        m = len(vec2)
        # Create grid of indices
        i_indices = np.repeat(np.arange(n), m)
        j_indices = np.tile(np.arange(m), n)
        # Compute products in parallel
        products = multiply(vec1[i_indices], vec2[j_indices])
        return products.reshape(n, m)
---

file: solver.py

---
import numpy as np
import numba

@numba.vectorize(['float64(float64, float64)'], nopython=True, target='parallel')
def multiply(a, b):
    return a * b

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        n = len(vec1)
        m = len(vec2)
        # Create grid of indices
        i_indices = np.repeat(np.arange(n), m)
        j_indices = np.tile(np.arange(m), n)
        # Compute products in parallel
        products = multiply(vec1[i_indices], vec2[j_indices])
        return products.reshape(n, m)

Language Model ✏️ Code Edit
The broadcasting method with `np.multiply.outer` achieved a 3.43x speedup. To further optimize, let's try using Numba's JIT compilation with fastmath and parallel execution:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-7
---
import numpy as np
import numba

@numba.njit(parallel=True, fastmath=True)
def outer_product(vec1, vec2):
    n = vec1.size
    m = vec2.size
    result = np.empty((n, m))
    for i in numba.prange(n):
        for j in range(m):
            result[i, j] = vec1[i] * vec2[j]
    return result

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        return outer_product(vec1, vec2)
---

import numpy as np
import numba

@numba.vectorize(['float64(float64, float64)'], nopython=True)
def multiply(a, b):
    return a * b

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        n = len(vec1)
        m = len(vec2)
        return multiply(vec1.reshape(n, 1), vec2.reshape(1, m))

@numba.vectorize(['float64(float64, float64)'], nopython=True)

        m = len(vec2)

- `edit`: Replace a range of lines with new content in a file. This is how you can create files: if the file does not exist, it will be created. Here is an example:
```
edit
file: <file_name>
lines: <start_line>-<end_line>
---
<new_content>
---
```

The command will:
1. Delete the lines from <start_line> to <end_line> (inclusive)
2. Insert <new_content> starting at <start_line>
3. If both <start_line> and <end_line> are 0, <new_content> will be prepended to the file

Example:
edit
file: solver.py
lines: 5-7
---

- `ls`: List all files in the current working directory.
- `view_file <file_name> [start_line]`: Display 100 lines of `<file_name>` starting from `start_line` (defaults to line 1).
- `revert`: Revert the code to the best-performing version thus far.
- `reference <string>`: Query the reference solver with a problem and receive its solution. If the problem's input is a list, this command would look like:
```
reference [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval_input <string>`: Run your current solver implementation on the given input. This is the only command that shows stdout from your solver along with both solutions. Example:
```
eval_input [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval`: Run evaluation on the current solution and report the results.
- `delete`: Delete a range of lines from a file using the format:
```
delete
file: <file_name>
lines: <start_line>-<end_line>

The command will delete the lines from <start_line> to <end_line> (inclusive)

Example:
```
delete
file: solver.py
lines: 5-10
```
- `profile <filename.py> <input>`: Profile your currently loaded solve method's performance on a given input. Shows the 25 most time-consuming lines. Requires specifying a python file (e.g., `solver.py`) for validation, though profiling runs on the current in-memory code.
Example:
```
profile solver.py [1, 2, 3]
```
- `profile_lines <filename.py> <line_number1, line_number2, ...> <input>`: Profiles the chosen lines of the currently loaded code on the given input. Requires specifying a python file for validation.
Example:
```
profile_lines solver.py 1,2,3 [1, 2, 3]
```

- `revert`: Revert the code to the best-performing version thus far.
- `reference <string>`: Query the reference solver with a problem and receive its solution. If the problem's input is a list, this command would look like:
```
reference [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval_input <string>`: Run your current solver implementation on the given input. This is the only command that shows stdout from your solver along with both solutions. Example:
```
eval_input [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval`: Run evaluation on the current solution and report the results.
- `delete`: Delete a range of lines from a file using the format:
```
delete
file: <file_name>
lines: <start_line>-<end_line>

The command will delete the lines from <start_line> to <end_line> (inclusive)

Example:
```
delete
file: solver.py
lines: 5-10
```
- `profile <filename.py> <input>`: Profile your currently loaded solve method's performance on a given input. Shows the 25 most time-consuming lines. Requires specifying a python file (e.g., `solver.py`) for validation, though profiling runs on the current in-memory code.
Example:
```
profile solver.py [1, 2, 3]
```
- `profile_lines <filename.py> <line_number1, line_number2, ...> <input>`: Profiles the chosen lines of the currently loaded code on the given input. Requires specifying a python file for validation.
Example:
```
profile_lines solver.py 1,2,3 [1, 2, 3]
```

---
```

The command will:
1. Delete the lines from <start_line> to <end_line> (inclusive)
2. Insert <new_content> starting at <start_line>
3. If both <start_line> and <end_line> are 0, <new_content> will be prepended to the file

Example:
edit
file: solver.py
lines: 5-7
---
def improved_function():
print("Optimized solution")
---
- `ls`: List all files in the current working directory.
- `view_file <file_name> [start_line]`: Display 100 lines of `<file_name>` starting from `start_line` (defaults to line 1).
- `revert`: Revert the code to the best-performing version thus far.
- `reference <string>`: Query the reference solver with a problem and receive its solution. If the problem's input is a list, this command would look like:
```
reference [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval_input <string>`: Run your current solver implementation on the given input. This is the only command that shows stdout from your solver along with both solutions. Example:
```
eval_input [1,2,3,4]
```
- `eval`: Run evaluation on the current solution and report the results.
- `delete`: Delete a range of lines from a file using the format:
```
delete
file: <file_name>
lines: <start_line>-<end_line>

The command will delete the lines from <start_line> to <end_line> (inclusive)

Example:
```
delete
file: solver.py
lines: 5-10
```
- `profile <filename.py> <input>`: Profile your currently loaded solve method's performance on a given input. Shows the 25 most time-consuming lines. Requires specifying a python file (e.g., `solver.py`) for validation, though profiling runs on the current in-memory code.
Example:
```
profile solver.py [1, 2, 3]
```
- `profile_lines <filename.py> <line_number1, line_number2, ...> <input>`: Profiles the chosen lines of the currently loaded code on the given input. Requires specifying a python file for validation.
Example:
```
profile_lines solver.py 1,2,3 [1, 2, 3]
```

The current implementation achieves a 3.46x speedup, which is good but not top-tier. Let's optimize further using Numba's parallel computation with explicit type declarations and fastmath:

edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-1000
---
import numpy as np
from numba import njit, prange

@njit('float64[:,:](float64[:], float64[:])', parallel=True, fastmath=True)
def numba_outer(vec1, vec2):
    n = vec1.size
    m = vec2.size
    result = np.empty((n, m), dtype=np.float64)
    for i in prange(n):
        for j in range(m):
            result[i, j] = vec1[i] * vec2[j]
    return result

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        # Ensure contiguous float64 arrays for optimal performance
        vec1 = np.ascontiguousarray(vec1, dtype=np.float64)
        vec2 = np.ascontiguousarray(vec2, dtype=np.float64)
        return numba_outer(vec1, vec2)

import numpy as np
from numba import njit, prange

@njit(parallel=True, fastmath=True)
def numba_outer(vec1, vec2):
    n = len(vec1)
    m = len(vec2)
    # Create index arrays and vectorize multiplication
    i_arr = np.arange(n)[:, np.newaxis]
    j_arr = np.arange(m)
    return vec1[i_arr] * vec2[j_arr]

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        # Ensure contiguous arrays for optimal performance
        vec1 = np.ascontiguousarray(vec1)
        vec2 = np.ascontiguousarray(vec2)
        return numba_outer(vec1, vec2)

The linter error persists because the static linter doesn't recognize Numba's `prange`. Let's modify the implementation to use a different approach that avoids `prange` while still being optimized. We'll use Numba's vectorized operations with parallel execution:

```
edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-1000
---
import numpy as np
from numba import njit, vectorize

@vectorize(['float64(float64, float64)'], nopython=True, fastmath=True, target='parallel')
def multiply(a, b):
return a * b

@njit(parallel=True, fastmath=True)
def numba_outer(vec1, vec2):
n = vec1.size
m = vec2.size
# Create expanded arrays for broadcasting
expanded_vec1 = np.repeat(vec1, m)
expanded_vec2 = np.tile(vec2, n)
# Compute element-wise multiplication
result = multiply(expanded_vec1, expanded_vec2)
return result.reshape(n, m)

class Solver:
def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
vec1, vec2 = problem
# Ensure contiguous arrays for optimal performance
vec1 = np.ascontiguousarray(vec1)
vec2 = np.ascontiguousarray(vec2)
return numba_outer(vec1, vec2)
---

import numpy as np
from numba import njit, vectorize

@vectorize(['float64(float64, float64)'], nopython=True, fastmath=True, target='parallel')
def multiply(a, b):
    return a * b

@njit(parallel=True, fastmath=True)
def numba_outer(vec1, vec2):
    n = vec1.size
    m = vec2.size
    # Create expanded arrays for broadcasting
    expanded_vec1 = np.repeat(vec1, m)
    expanded_vec2 = np.tile(vec2, n)
    # Compute element-wise multiplication
    result = multiply(expanded_vec1, expanded_vec2)
    return result.reshape(n, m)

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        # Ensure contiguous arrays for optimal performance
        vec1 = np.ascontiguousarray(vec1)
        vec2 = np.ascontiguousarray(vec2)
        return numba_outer(vec1, vec2)

The broadcasting implementation provides a solid 3.45x speedup over the reference solution. This is likely near the optimal performance we can achieve with pure NumPy. To further optimize, let's try using Numba with a simple nested loop implementation that avoids `prange` to prevent linter issues:

```
edit
file: solver.py
lines: 1-1000
---
import numpy as np
from numba import njit

@njit(fastmath=True)
def outer_product(vec1, vec2):
n = vec1.size
m = vec2.size
out = np.empty((n, m), dtype=vec1.dtype)
for i in range(n):
for j in range(m):
out[i, j] = vec1[i] * vec2[j]
return out

class Solver:
def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
vec1, vec2 = problem
# Ensure contiguous arrays for optimal performance
vec1 = np.ascontiguousarray(vec1)
vec2 = np.ascontiguousarray(vec2)
return outer_product(vec1, vec2)
---

import numpy as np

class Solver:
    def solve(self, problem, **kwargs):
        vec1, vec2 = problem
        return vec1.reshape(-1, 1) * vec2
